          Plataea

          Sea forces
         </b>
         :
         <br/>
         400 triremes
         <br/>
         6,000 marines
         <br/>
         68,000 oarsmen
         <br/>
         <br/>
         <b>
          Total
         </b>
         :
         <br/>
         125,000 men
         <br/>
         400 ships
        </td>

           Land forces
          </b>
          : 100,000 soldiers or less (modern estimates)
         </p>
         <p>
          <b>
           Sea forces
          </b>
          : 600 - 1200 ships (modern estimates)
         </p>
         <br/>
         <b>
          Total
         </b>
         :
         <br/>
         300,000–500,000 (modern estimates)

      <p>
       The
       <b>
        second
        <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">
         Persian
        </a>
        invasion of
        <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">
         Greece
        </a>
       </b>
       (480–479 BC) occurred during the
       <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars" title="Greco-Persian Wars">
        Greco-Persian Wars
       </a>
       , as King
       <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">
        Xerxes I
       </a>
       of Persia sought to conquer all of Greece. The invasion was a direct, if delayed, response to the defeat of the
       <a href="/wiki/First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="First Persian invasion of Greece">
        first Persian invasion of Greece
       </a>
       (492–490 BC) at the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Marathon" title="Battle of Marathon">
        Battle of Marathon
       </a>
       , which ended
       <a href="/wiki/Darius_I" title="Darius I">
        Darius I
       </a>
       's attempts to subjugate Greece. After Darius's death, his son Xerxes spent several years planning for the second invasion, mustering an enormous army and navy. The
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Athens" title="History of Athens">
        Athenians
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">
        Spartans
       </a>
       led the Greek resistance. About a tenth of the Greek city-states joined the 'Allied' effort; most remained neutral or submitted to Xerxes.
      </p>
      <p>
       The invasion began in spring 480 BC, when the Persian army crossed the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellespont" title="Hellespont">
        Hellespont
       </a>
       and marched through
       <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">
        Thrace
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">
        Macedon
       </a>
       to
       <a href="/wiki/Thessaly" title="Thessaly">
        Thessaly
       </a>
       . The Persian advance was blocked at the pass of
       <a href="/wiki/Thermopylae" title="Thermopylae">
        Thermopylae
       </a>
       by a small Allied force under King
       <a href="/wiki/Leonidas_I" title="Leonidas I">
        Leonidas I
       </a>
       of Sparta; simultaneously, the Persian fleet was blocked by an Allied fleet at the straits of
       <a href="/wiki/Artemisium" title="Artemisium">
        Artemisium
       </a>
       . At the famous
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Thermopylae" title="Battle of Thermopylae">
        Battle of Thermopylae
       </a>
       , the Allied army held back the Persian army for seven days, before they were outflanked by a mountain path and the Allied rearguard was trapped and annihilated. The Allied fleet had also withstood two days of Persian attacks at the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Artemisium" title="Battle of Artemisium">
        Battle of Artemisium
       </a>
       , but when news reached them of the disaster at Thermopylae, they withdrew to
       <a href="/wiki/Salamis_Island" title="Salamis Island">
        Salamis
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       After Thermopylae, all of
       <a href="/wiki/Boeotia" title="Boeotia">
        Boeotia
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Attica" title="Attica">
        Attica
       </a>
       fell to the Persian army, which captured and burnt
       <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">
        Athens
       </a>
       . However, a larger Allied army fortified the narrow
       <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Corinth" title="Isthmus of Corinth">
        Isthmus of Corinth
       </a>
       , protecting the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peloponnesus" title="Peloponnesus">
        Peloponnesus
       </a>
       from Persian conquest. Both sides thus sought a naval victory that might decisively alter the course of the war. The Athenian general
       <a href="/wiki/Themistocles" title="Themistocles">
        Themistocles
       </a>
       succeeded in luring the Persian navy into the narrow Straits of Salamis, where the huge number of Persian ships became disorganised, and were soundly beaten by the Allied fleet. The Allied
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Salamis" title="Battle of Salamis">
        victory at Salamis
       </a>
       prevented a quick conclusion to the invasion, and fearing becoming trapped in Europe, Xerxes retreated to Asia leaving his general
       <a href="/wiki/Mardonius" title="Mardonius">
        Mardonius
       </a>
       to finish the conquest with the elite of the army.
      </p>
      <p>
       The following spring, the Allies assembled the largest ever
       <a href="/wiki/Hoplite" title="Hoplite">
        hoplite
       </a>
       army, and marched north from the isthmus to confront Mardonius. At the ensuing
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Plataea" title="Battle of Plataea">
        Battle of Plataea
       </a>
       , the Greek infantry again proved its superiority, inflicting a severe defeat on the Persians and killing Mardonius in the process. On the same day, across the
       <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">
        Aegean Sea
       </a>
       an Allied navy destroyed the remnants of the Persian navy at the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mycale" title="Battle of Mycale">
        Battle of Mycale
       </a>
       . With this double defeat, the invasion was ended, and Persian power in the Aegean severely dented. The Greeks would now move to the
       <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars#Greek_counterattack_(479–478_BC)" title="Greco-Persian Wars">
        offensive
       </a>
       , eventually expelling the Persians from Europe, the Aegean islands and Ionia before the war finally came to an end in 479 BC.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">
        Sources
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Sources">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">
        Herodotus
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The main source for the Great Greco-Persian Wars is the Greek historian
       <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">
        Herodotus
       </a>
       . Herodotus, who has been called the 'Father of History',
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       was born in 484 BC in Halicarnassus, Asia Minor (then under Persian overlordship). He wrote his 'Enquiries' (Greek—
       <i>
        Historia
       </i>
       ; English—
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Histories_(Herodotus)" title="Histories (Herodotus)">
         (The) Histories
        </a>
       </i>
       ) around 440–430 BC, trying to trace the origins of the Greco-Persian Wars, which would still have been relatively recent history (the wars finally ending in 450 BC).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hxvi_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-hxvi-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Herodotus's approach was entirely novel, and at least in Western society, he does seem to have invented 'history' as we know it.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hxvi_4-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-hxvi-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As Holland has it: "For the first time, a chronicler set himself to trace the origins of a conflict not to a past so remote so as to be utterly fabulous, nor to the whims and wishes of some god, nor to a people's claim to manifest destiny, but rather explanations he could verify personally."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hxvi_4-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-hxvi-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Some subsequent ancient historians, despite following in his footsteps, criticised Herodotus, starting with
       <a href="/wiki/Thucydides" title="Thucydides">
        Thucydides
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fin15_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Fin15-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Nevertheless, Thucydides chose to begin his history where Herodotus left off (at the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Sestos" title="Siege of Sestos">
        Siege of Sestos
       </a>
       ), and therefore evidently felt that Herodotus's history was accurate enough not to need re-writing or correcting.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fin15_6-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Fin15-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">
        Plutarch
       </a>
       criticised Herodotus in his essay "On The Malignity of Herodotus", describing Herodotus as "
       <i>
        Philobarbaros
       </i>
       " (barbarian-lover), for not being pro-Greek enough, which suggests that Herodotus might actually have done a reasonable job of being even-handed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A negative view of Herodotus was passed on to Renaissance Europe, though he remained well read.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, since the 19th century his reputation has been dramatically rehabilitated by archaeological finds that have repeatedly confirmed his version of events.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h377_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h377-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The prevailing modern view is that Herodotus generally did a remarkable job in his
       <i>
        Historia
       </i>
       , but that some of his specific details (particularly troop numbers and dates) should be viewed with skepticism.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h377_9-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h377-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Nevertheless, there are still some historians who believe Herodotus made up much of his story.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Greek historian
       <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">
        Diodorus Siculus
       </a>
       from Sicily, writing in the 1st century BC in his
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Bibliotheca_historica" title="Bibliotheca historica">
         Bibliotheca Historica
        </a>
       </i>
       , also provides an account of the Greco-Persian wars, partially derived from the earlier Greek historian
       <a href="/wiki/Ephorus" title="Ephorus">
        Ephorus
       </a>
       . This account is fairly consistent with Herodotus's.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Greco-Persian wars are also described in less detail by a number of other ancient historians including Plutarch,
       <a href="/wiki/Ctesias" title="Ctesias">
        Ctesias
       </a>
       , and are alluded by other authors, such as the playwright
       <a href="/wiki/Aeschylus" title="Aeschylus">
        Aeschylus
       </a>
       . Archaeological evidence, such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Serpent_Column" title="Serpent Column">
        Serpent Column
       </a>
       , also supports some of Herodotus's specific claims.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Background">
        Background
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Background">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars" title="Greco-Persian Wars">
        Greco-Persian Wars
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="First Persian invasion of Greece">
        First Persian invasion of Greece
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Greek city-states of Athens and
       <a href="/wiki/Eretria" title="Eretria">
        Eretria
       </a>
       had supported the unsuccessful
       <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt" title="Ionian Revolt">
        Ionian Revolt
       </a>
       against the Persian Empire of
       <a href="/wiki/Darius_I" title="Darius I">
        Darius I
       </a>
       in 499–494 BC. The Persian Empire was still relatively young, and prone to revolts among its subject peoples.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h47_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h47-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h203_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h203-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Moreover, Darius was a usurper, and had spent considerable time extinguishing revolts against his rule.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h47_13-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h47-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Ionian revolt threatened the integrity of his empire, and Darius thus vowed to punish those involved (especially those not already part of the empire).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h171_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h171-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Darius also saw the opportunity to expand his empire into the fractious world of Ancient Greece.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h171_16-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h171-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A preliminary expedition under Mardonius, in 492 BC, to secure the land approaches to Greece ended with the re-conquest of
       <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">
        Thrace
       </a>
       and forced
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">
        Macedon
       </a>
       to become a fully subordinate kingdom part of Persia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2011135-138,_342-345_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2011135-138,_342-345-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It had previously been a vassal as early as the late 6th century BC, but remained having autonomy and not fully subordinate yet.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2011135-138,_342-345_18-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2011135-138,_342-345-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="793" data-file-width="992" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg/300px-Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg/450px-Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg/600px-Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg.png 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A map showing the Greek world at the time of the invasion
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 491 BC, Darius sent emissaries to all the Greek city-states, asking for a gift of '
       <a href="/wiki/Earth_and_water" title="Earth and water">
        earth and water
       </a>
       ' in token of their submission to him.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h178_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h178-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Having had a demonstration of his power the previous year, the majority of Greek cities duly obliged. In Athens, however, the ambassadors were put on trial and then executed; in Sparta, they were simply thrown down a well.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h178_19-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h178-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This meant that Sparta was also now effectively at war with Persia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h178_19-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-h178-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Darius thus put together an ambitious task force under
       <a href="/wiki/Datis" title="Datis">
        Datis
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Artaphernes_(son_of_Artaphernes)" title="Artaphernes (son of Artaphernes)">
        Artaphernes
       </a>
       in 490 BC, which attacked
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naxos_Island" title="Naxos Island">
        Naxos
       </a>
       , before receiving the submission of the other
       <a href="/wiki/Cyclades" title="Cyclades">
        Cycladic Islands
       </a>
       . The task force then moved on Eretria, which it besieged and destroyed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Finally, it moved to attack Athens, landing at the bay of
       <a href="/wiki/Marathon,_Greece" title="Marathon, Greece">
        Marathon
       </a>
       , where it was met by a heavily outnumbered Athenian army. At the ensuing Battle of Marathon, the Athenians won a remarkable victory, which resulted in the withdrawal of the Persian army to Asia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Darius therefore began raising a huge new army with which he meant to completely subjugate Greece; however, in 486 BC, his
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">
        Egyptian
       </a>
       subjects revolted, indefinitely postponing any Greek expedition.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h203_14-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h203-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Darius then died while preparing to march on Egypt, and the throne of Persia passed to his son
       <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">
        Xerxes I
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Xerxes crushed the Egyptian revolt, and very quickly restarted the preparations for the invasion of Greece.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h208_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h208-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Persian_preparations">
        Persian preparations
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Persian preparations">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Since this was to be a full-scale invasion, it required long-term planning, stock-piling and conscription.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h208_23-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h208-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was decided that
       <a href="/wiki/Xerxes%27_Pontoon_Bridges" title="Xerxes' Pontoon Bridges">
        Xerxes' Pontoon Bridges
       </a>
       were to be set up to allow his army to cross the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellespont" title="Hellespont">
        Hellespont
       </a>
       to Europe, and that
       <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_Canal" title="Xerxes Canal">
        a canal
       </a>
       should be dug across the isthmus of
       <a href="/wiki/Mount_Athos" title="Mount Athos">
        Mount Athos
       </a>
       (rounding which headland, a Persian fleet had been destroyed in 492 BC).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h213_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h213-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These were both feats of exceptional ambition, which would have been beyond any contemporary state.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h213_24-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h213-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, the campaign was delayed one year because of another revolt in Egypt and
       <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">
        Babylonia
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 481 BC, after roughly four years of preparation, Xerxes began to muster the troops for the invasion of Europe. Herodotus gives the names of 46 nations from which troops were drafted.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Persian army was gathered in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">
        Asia Minor
       </a>
       in the summer and autumn of 481 BC. The armies from the Eastern satrapies was gathered in
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kritala&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kritala (page does not exist)">
        Kritala
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Cappadocia" title="Cappadocia">
        Cappadocia
       </a>
       and were led by Xerxes to
       <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">
        Sardis
       </a>
       where they passed the winter.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Early in spring it moved to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abydos,_Hellespont" title="Abydos, Hellespont">
        Abydos
       </a>
       where it was joined with the armies of the western satrapies.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Then the army that Xerxes had mustered marched towards Europe, crossing the Hellespont on two
       <a href="/wiki/Pontoon_bridge" title="Pontoon bridge">
        pontoon bridges
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Size_of_the_Persian_forces">
        Size of the Persian forces
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Size of the Persian forces">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Army">
        Army
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Army">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crossing_the_Hellespont_by_Xerxes_with_his_huge_army.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="349" data-file-width="552" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Crossing_the_Hellespont_by_Xerxes_with_his_huge_army.gif/220px-Crossing_the_Hellespont_by_Xerxes_with_his_huge_army.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Crossing_the_Hellespont_by_Xerxes_with_his_huge_army.gif/330px-Crossing_the_Hellespont_by_Xerxes_with_his_huge_army.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Crossing_the_Hellespont_by_Xerxes_with_his_huge_army.gif/440px-Crossing_the_Hellespont_by_Xerxes_with_his_huge_army.gif 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crossing_the_Hellespont_by_Xerxes_with_his_huge_army.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Crossing the Hellespont by Xerxes with his huge army
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The numbers of troops that Xerxes mustered for the second invasion of Greece have been the subject of endless dispute, because the numbers given in ancient sources are very large indeed. Herodotus claimed that there were, in total, 2.5 million military personnel, accompanied by an equivalent number of support personnel.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VII,_186_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-VII,_186-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The poet
       <a href="/wiki/Simonides_of_Ceos" title="Simonides of Ceos">
        Simonides
       </a>
       , who was a contemporary, talks of four million;
       <a href="/wiki/Ctesias" title="Ctesias">
        Ctesias
       </a>
       , based on Persian records, gave 800,000 as the total number of the army (without the support personnel) that was assembled by Xerxes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ctes_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ctes-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While it has been suggested that Herodotus or his sources had access to official Persian Empire records of the forces involved in the expedition, modern scholars tend to reject these figures based on knowledge of the Persian military systems, their logistical capabilities, the Greek countryside, and supplies available along the army's route.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-souza_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-souza-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Modern scholars thus generally attribute the numbers given in the ancient sources to the result of miscalculations or exaggerations on the part of the victors, or disinformation by the Persians in the run up to the war.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h237_2-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h237-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The topic has been hotly debated but the modern consensus revolves around the figure of 300,000–500,000.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-souza_1-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-souza-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h237_2-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-h237-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Nevertheless, whatever the real numbers were, it is clear that Xerxes was anxious to ensure a successful expedition by mustering overwhelming numerical superiority by land and by sea,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-souza_1-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-souza-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and also that much of the army died of starvation and disease, never returning to Asia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VIII115_32-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-VIII115-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Herodotus tells us that the army and navy, while moving through Thrace, was halted at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doriskos" title="Doriskos">
        Doriskos
       </a>
       for an inspection by Xerxes, and he recounts the numbers of troops found to be present:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

          <a href="#cite_note-vii-78-56">
           [53]
          </a>
         </sup>
         <a href="/wiki/Trabzon" title="Trabzon">
          Mares
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Colchis" title="Colchis">
          Colchians
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Urartu" title="Urartu">
          Alarodians
         </a>
         ,
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Kurdish_people" title="History of the Kurdish people">
          Saspirians
         </a>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii=79_57-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-vii=79-57">
           [54]
          </a>
         </sup>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">
          Red Sea
         </a>
         islanders.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-80_58-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-vii-80-58">
           [55]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
        <td>
         1,700,000
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-60_59-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-vii-60-59">
           [56]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Horse cavalry from the
         <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">
          Persians
         </a>
         ,
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-84_60-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-vii-84-60">
           [57]
          </a>
         </sup>
         <a href="/wiki/Sagartians" title="Sagartians">
          Sagartians
         </a>
         ,
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-85_61-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-vii-85-61">
           [58]
          </a>
         </sup>
         <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">
          Medes
         </a>
         ,
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khuzistan" title="Khuzistan">
          Cissians
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">
          Indians
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Caspians" title="Caspians">
          Caspians
         </a>
         and
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balochistan_(Pakistan)" title="Balochistan (Pakistan)">
          Paricanians
         </a>
         .
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-86_62-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-vii-86-62">
           [59]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
        <td>
         80,000
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-87_63-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-vii-87-63">
           [60]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">
          Arab
         </a>
         camel troops and
         <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">
          Libyan
         </a>
         charioteers
        </td>
        <td>
         20,000
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-184_36-4">

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>

       <tr>

         </a>
         : European
         <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">

         </a>
         ,
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paionia" title="Paionia">

         <a href="/wiki/Eordea" title="Eordea">

         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-185_66-1">

           [62]
          </a>

        </td>

        <td>
         <b>
          Grand Total
         </b>
        </td>

         <b>
          2,641,610
         </b>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
       <div class="mw-references-wrap">
        <ol class="references">
         <li id="cite_note-a-37">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-a_37-0">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           The 30 marines are in addition to the figure of 200 given for the ships' crews
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-b-39">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-b_39-0">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           There is some contradiction in Herodotus's accounts. The figure of 240,000 is derived from 3,000
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penteconter_(ship)" title="Penteconter (ship)">
            penteconters
           </a>
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-c-40">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-c_40-0">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           The 47th ethnic group is missing from Herodotus's text.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-d-64">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-d_64-0">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           The term "Asian" is Herodotus' but under that term he also includes Arabians and north Africans.
          </span>
         </li>
        </ol>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Herodotus doubles this number to account for support personnel and thus he reports that the whole army numbered 5,283,220 men.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VII,_186_30-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-VII,_186-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Other ancient sources give similarly large numbers. The poet
       <a href="/wiki/Simonides_of_Ceos" title="Simonides of Ceos">
        Simonides
       </a>
       , who was a near-contemporary, talks of four million;
       <a href="/wiki/Ctesias" title="Ctesias">
        Ctesias
       </a>
       gave 800,000 as the total number of the army that assembled in Doriskos.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ctes_31-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ctes-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       An early and very influential modern historian,
       <a href="/wiki/George_Grote" title="George Grote">
        George Grote
       </a>
       , set the tone by expressing incredulity at the numbers given by Herodotus:
       <i>
        "To admit this overwhelming total, or anything near to it, is obviously impossible."
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grote_71-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-grote-71">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Grote's main objection is the supply problem, though he does not analyse the problem in detail. He did not reject Herodotus's account altogether, citing the latter's reporting of the Persians' careful methods of accounting and their stockpiling of supply caches for three years, but drew attention to the contradictions in the ancient sources.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grote_71-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-grote-71">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A later influential historian, J. B. Bury, calls Herodotus's numbers "wholly fabulous" and judges that the Persian land forces may have been 180,000.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A major limiting factor for the size of the Persian army, first suggested by Sir Frederick Maurice (a British transport officer) is the supply of water.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mau_73-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Mau-73">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Maurice suggested in the region of 200,000 men and 70,000 animals could have been supported by the rivers in that region of Greece. He further suggested that Herodotus may have confused the Persian terms for
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chiliarchy" title="Chiliarchy">
        chiliarchy
       </a>
       (1,000) and myriarchy (10,000), leading to an exaggeration by a factor of ten.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mau_73-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Mau-73">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Other early modern scholars estimated that the land forces participating in the invasion at 100,000 soldiers or less, based on the logistical systems available to the Ancients.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scott_77-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Scott-77">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Munro and
       <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Walter_Macan" title="Reginald Walter Macan">
        Macan
       </a>
       note Herodotus giving the names of six major commanders and 29 myriarchs (leaders of a
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Baivabaram&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Baivabaram (page does not exist)">
        baivabaram
       </a>
       , the basic unit of the Persian infantry, which numbered about 10,000-strong
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ); this would give a land force of roughly 300,000 men.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Other proponents of larger numbers suggest figures from 250,000 to 700,000.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
        <a href="#cite_note-86">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
        <a href="#cite_note-87">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Fleet">
        Fleet
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Fleet">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The size of the Persian fleet is also disputed, though perhaps less so.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h394_88-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h394-88">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to Herodotus the Persian fleet numbered 1,207 triremes and 3,000 transport and supply ships, including 50-oared galleys (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Galley#Penteconters" title="Galley">
         Penteconters
        </a>
       </i>
       ) (πεντηκοντήρ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VII,_97_38-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-VII,_97-38">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Herodotus gives a detailed breakdown of the Persian triremes:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Region
        </th>
        <th>
         Number
         <br/>
         of ships

         of ships

        <th>

       <tr>

         and
         <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">
          Syria
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         300
        </td>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">
          Egypt
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         200
        </td>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">
          Cyprus

        </td>
       </tr>

         <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">

        </td>

         100
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>

          Caria
         </a>
        </td>

        </td>

        </td>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Lycia" title="Lycia">
          Lycia
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Pamphylia" title="Pamphylia">
          Pamphylia
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         30
        </td>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Dorians" title="Dorians">
          Dorians
         </a>
         from
         <br/>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">
          Asia Minor
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         30
        </td>
        <td>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cycladic_Islands" title="Cycladic Islands">
          Cyclades
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         17
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          Total
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          1207
         </b>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Herodotus also records that this was the number at the Battle of Salamis, despite the losses earlier in storms off Sepia and Euboea, and at the battle of Artemisium. He claims that the losses were replenished with reinforcements, though he only records 120 triremes from the Greeks of Thrace and an unspecified number of ships from the Greek islands.
       <a href="/wiki/Aeschylus" title="Aeschylus">
        Aeschylus
       </a>
       , who fought at Salamis, also claims that he faced 1,207 warships there, of which 1,000 were triremes and 207 fast ships.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aesch_89-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Aesch-89">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">
        Diodorus
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
        <a href="#cite_note-90">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Lysias" title="Lysias">
        Lysias
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
        <a href="#cite_note-91">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       independently claim there were 1,200 at Doriskos. The number of 1,207 (for the outset only) is also given by
       <a href="/wiki/Ephorus" title="Ephorus">
        Ephorus
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
        <a href="#cite_note-92">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       while his teacher
       <a href="/wiki/Isocrates" title="Isocrates">
        Isocrates
       </a>
       claims there were 1,300 at Doriskos and 1,200 at Salamis.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
        <a href="#cite_note-93">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
        <a href="#cite_note-94">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ctesias gives another number, 1,000 ships,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ctes_31-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-ctes-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       while
       <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">
        Plato
       </a>
       , speaking in general terms refers to 1,000 ships and more.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
        <a href="#cite_note-95">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       These numbers are (by ancient standards) consistent, and this could be interpreted that a number around 1,200 is correct. Among modern scholars some have accepted this number, although suggesting that the number must have been lower by the Battle of Salamis.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
        <a href="#cite_note-96">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
        <a href="#cite_note-97">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L93_98-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-L93-98">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Other recent works on the Persian Wars reject this number—1,207 being seen as more of a reference to the combined Greek fleet in the
       <a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">
        Iliad
       </a>
       —and generally claim that the Persians could have launched no more than around 600 warships into the Aegean.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L93_98-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-L93-98">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
        <a href="#cite_note-99">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
        <a href="#cite_note-100">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Greek_preparations">
        Greek preparations
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Greek preparations">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The Athenians had been preparing for war with the Persians since the mid-480s BC, and in 482 BC the decision was taken, under the guidance of the politician Themistocles, to build a massive fleet of triremes that would be necessary for the Greeks to fight the Persians.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h217_101-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h217-101">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Athenians did not have the man-power to fight on land and sea; therefore combatting the Persians would require an alliance of several Greek city states. In 481 BC Xerxes sent ambassadors around Greece asking for earth and water, but making the very deliberate omission of Athens and Sparta.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
        <a href="#cite_note-102">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Support thus began to coalesce around these two states.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenic_alliance">
        Hellenic alliance
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Hellenic alliance">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       A congress of states met at
       <a href="/wiki/Corinth" title="Corinth">
        Corinth
       </a>
       in late autumn of 481 BC, and a confederate alliance of
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Greece" title="History of Greece">
        Greek city-states
       </a>
       was formed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-145_103-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-vii-145-103">
         [99]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This confederation had the power to send envoys asking for assistance and to dispatch troops from the member states to defensive points after joint consultation. Herodotus does not formulate an abstract name for the union but simply calls them "οἱ Ἕλληνες" (the Greeks) and "the Greeks who had sworn alliance" (Godley translation) or "the Greeks who had banded themselves together" (Rawlinson translation).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
        <a href="#cite_note-104">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Hereafter, they will be referred to as the 'Allies'. Sparta and Athens had a leading role in the congress but interests of all the states played a part in determining defensive strategy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vii-160_105-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-vii-160-105">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Little is known about the internal workings of the congress or the discussions during its meetings. Only 70 of the approximately 700 Greek cities sent representatives. Nevertheless, this was remarkable for the disjointed Greek world, especially since many of the city-states in attendance were still technically at war with each other.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h226_106-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h226-106">
         [102]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The majority of other city-states remained more-or-less neutral, awaiting the outcome of the confrontation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holland,_p225_107-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Holland,_p225-107">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thebes_(Greece)" title="Thebes (Greece)">
        Thebes
       </a>
       was a major absentee, and was suspected of being willing to aid the Persians once the invasion force arrived.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holland,_p225_107-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Holland,_p225-107">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h263_108-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h263-108">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Not all Thebans agreed with this policy, and 400 "loyalist" hoplites joined the Allied force at Thermopylae (at least according to one possible interpretation).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h263_108-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h263-108">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The most notable city actively siding with the Persians ("Medised") was
       <a href="/wiki/Argos" title="Argos">
        Argos
       </a>
       , in the otherwise Spartan-dominated Peloponnese.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
        <a href="#cite_note-109">
         [105]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h168_110-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h168-110">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, the Argives had been severely weakened in 494 BC, when a Spartan-force led by
       <a href="/wiki/Cleomenes_I" title="Cleomenes I">
        Cleomenes I
       </a>
       had annihilated the Argive army in
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sepeia" title="Battle of Sepeia">
        Battle of Sepeia
       </a>
       and then massacred the fugitives.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h168_110-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h168-110">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Size_of_allied_forces">
        Size of allied forces
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Size of allied forces">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Thermopylae" title="Battle of Thermopylae">
        Battle of Thermopylae
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Artemisium" title="Battle of Artemisium">
        Battle of Artemisium
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Salamis" title="Battle of Salamis">
        Battle of Salamis
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Plataea" title="Battle of Plataea">
        Battle of Plataea
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mycale" title="Battle of Mycale">
        Battle of Mycale
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The allies had no 'standing army', nor was there any requirement to form one; since they were fighting on home territory, they could muster armies as and when required. Different-sized allied forces thus appeared throughout the campaign. These numbers are discussed fully in the article for each battle.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span id="Spring_480_BC:_Thrace.2C_Macedonia_and_Thessaly">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Spring_480_BC:_Thrace,_Macedonia_and_Thessaly">
        Spring 480 BC: Thrace, Macedonia and Thessaly
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Spring 480 BC: Thrace, Macedonia and Thessaly">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Having crossed into Europe in April 480 BC, the Persian army began its march to Greece. Five major food depots had been set up along the path: at
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Leuke_Akte&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Leuke Akte (page does not exist)">
        White Headland
       </a>
       on the Thracian side of the Hellespont, at
       <a href="/wiki/Tyrodiza" title="Tyrodiza">
        Tyrodiza
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Marmara_Ere%C4%9Flisi" title="Marmara Ereğlisi">
        Perinthian
       </a>
       territory, at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doriskos" title="Doriskos">
        Doriskos
       </a>
       at the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evros_River" title="Evros River">
        Evros river
       </a>
       estuary where the Asian army was linked up with the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balkan" title="Balkan">
        Balkan
       </a>
       allies, at
       <a href="/wiki/Eion" title="Eion">
        Eion
       </a>
       on the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strymon_River" title="Strymon River">
        Strymon river
       </a>
       , and at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Therme" title="Therme">
        Therme
       </a>
       , modern-day
       <a href="/wiki/Thessaloniki" title="Thessaloniki">
        Thessaloniki
       </a>
       . There, food had been sent from Asia for several years in preparation for the campaign. Animals had been bought and fattened, while the local populations had, for several months, been ordered to grind the grains into flour.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
        <a href="#cite_note-111">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Persian army took roughly three months to travel unopposed from the Hellespont to Therme, a journey of about 600 km (360 mi). It paused at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doriskos" title="Doriskos">
        Doriskos
       </a>
       where it was joined by the fleet. Xerxes reorganized the troops into tactical units replacing the national formations used earlier for the march.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
        <a href="#cite_note-112">
         [108]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Allied 'congress' met again in the spring of 480 BC. A Thessalian delegation suggested that the allies could muster in the narrow
       <a href="/wiki/Vale_of_Tempe" title="Vale of Tempe">
        Vale of Tempe
       </a>
       , on the borders of
       <a href="/wiki/Thessaly" title="Thessaly">
        Thessaly
       </a>
       , and thereby block Xerxes's advance.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h248_113-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h248-113">
         [109]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A force of 10,000 Allies led by the Spartan
       <a href="/wiki/Polemarch" title="Polemarch">
        polemarch
       </a>
       Euenetus and Themistocles was thus despatched to the pass. However, once there, they were warned by
       <a href="/wiki/Alexander_I_of_Macedon" title="Alexander I of Macedon">
        Alexander I of Macedon
       </a>
       that the vale could be bypassed by at least two other passes, and that the army of Xerxes was overwhelming; the Allies therefore retreated.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VII173_114-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-VII173-114">
         [110]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Shortly afterwards, they received the news that Xerxes had crossed the Hellespont.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VII173_114-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-VII173-114">
         [110]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The abandonment of Tempe meant that all of Thessaly submitted to the Persians, as did many cities to the north of the pass of
       <a href="/wiki/Thermopylae" title="Thermopylae">
        Thermopylae
       </a>
       when it seemed help was not forthcoming.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VII174_115-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-VII174-115">
         [111]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A second strategy was therefore suggested to the Allies by
       <a href="/wiki/Themistocles" title="Themistocles">
        Themistocles
       </a>
       . The route to southern Greece (Boeotia, Attica and the Peloponnesus) would require the army of Xerxes to travel through the very narrow pass of
       <a href="/wiki/Thermopylae" title="Thermopylae">
        Thermopylae
       </a>
       . This could easily be blocked by the Allies, despite the overwhelming number of Persians. Furthermore, to prevent the Persians bypassing Thermopylae by sea, the allied navy could block the straits of
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Artemisium" title="Battle of Artemisium">
        Artemisium
       </a>
       . This dual strategy was adopted by the congress.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h255_116-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h255-116">
         [112]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, the Peloponnesian cities made fall-back plans to defend the Isthmus of Corinth should it come to it, while the women and children of Athens were evacuated
       <i>
        en masse
       </i>
       to the Peloponnesian city of
       <a href="/wiki/Troezen" title="Troezen">
        Troezen
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
        <a href="#cite_note-117">
         [113]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="August_480_BC:_Thermopylae_and_Artemisium">
        August 480 BC: Thermopylae and Artemisium
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: August 480 BC: Thermopylae and Artemisium">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Thermopylae" title="Battle of Thermopylae">
        Battle of Thermopylae
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Artemisium" title="Battle of Artemisium">
        Battle of Artemisium
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       When the Allies received the news that Xerxes was clearing paths around
       <a href="/wiki/Mount_Olympus" title="Mount Olympus">
        Mount Olympus
       </a>
       , and thus intending to march towards Thermopylae, it was both the period of truce that accompanied the
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Olympic_Games" title="Ancient Olympic Games">
        Olympic games
       </a>
       , and the Spartan festival of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carneia" title="Carneia">
        Carneia
       </a>
       , during both of which warfare was considered sacrilegious.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h257_118-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h257-118">
         [114]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Nevertheless, the Spartans considered the threat so grave that they despatched their king
       <a href="/wiki/Leonidas_I" title="Leonidas I">
        Leonidas I
       </a>
       with his personal bodyguard (the
       <i>
        Hippeis
       </i>
       ) of 300 men (in this case, the elite young soldiers in the Hippeis were replaced by veterans who already had sons).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h257_118-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h257-118">
         [114]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Leonidas was supported by contingents from the Peloponnesian cities allied to Sparta, and other forces that were picked up
       <i>
        en route
       </i>
       to Thermopylae.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h257_118-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-h257-118">
         [114]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Allies proceeded to occupy the pass, rebuilt the wall the
       <a href="/wiki/Phocis_(ancient_region)" title="Phocis (ancient region)">
        Phocians
       </a>
       had built at the narrowest point of the pass, and waited for Xerxes's arrival.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h262_119-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h262-119">
         [115]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thermopylae_ancient_coastline_large.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Thermopylae_ancient_coastline_large.jpg/300px-Thermopylae_ancient_coastline_large.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Thermopylae_ancient_coastline_large.jpg/450px-Thermopylae_ancient_coastline_large.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Thermopylae_ancient_coastline_large.jpg/600px-Thermopylae_ancient_coastline_large.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thermopylae_ancient_coastline_large.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The pass of Thermopylae in modern times
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       When the Persians arrived at Thermopylae in mid-August, they initially waited for three days for the Allies to disperse. When Xerxes was eventually persuaded that the Allies intended to contest the pass, he sent his troops to attack.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
        <a href="#cite_note-120">
         [116]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, the Greek position was ideally suited to
       <a href="/wiki/Hoplite" title="Hoplite">
        hoplite
       </a>
       warfare, the Persian contingents being forced to attack the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phalanx_formation" title="Phalanx formation">
        phalanx
       </a>
       head on.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121">
        <a href="#cite_note-121">
         [117]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Allies thus withstood two full days of battle and everything Xerxes could throw at them. However, at the end of the second day, they were betrayed by a local resident named
       <a href="/wiki/Ephialtes_of_Trachis" title="Ephialtes of Trachis">
        Ephialtes
       </a>
       who revealed to Xerxes a mountain path that led behind the Allied lines. Xerxes then sent his elite guards, the Immortals on a night march to outflank the Allied. When he was made aware of this maneuver (while the Immortals were still
       <i>
        en route
       </i>
       ), Leonidas dismissed the bulk of the Allied army, remaining to guard the rear with 300 Spartans, 700 Thespians, 400 Thebians and perhaps a few hundred others. On the third day of the battle, the remaining Allies sallied forth from the wall to meet the Persians and slaughter as many as they could.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VII223_122-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-VII223-122">
         [118]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ultimately, however, the Allied rearguard was annihilated, and the pass of Thermopylae opened to the Persians.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VII225_123-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-VII225-123">
         [119]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Simultaneous with the battle at Thermopylae, an Allied naval force of 271 triremes defended the Straits of Artemisium against the Persians.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VIII2_124-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-VIII2-124">
         [120]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Directly before Artemisium, the Persian fleet had been caught in a gale off the coast of
       <a href="/wiki/Magnesia_Prefecture" title="Magnesia Prefecture">
        Magnesia
       </a>
       , losing many ships, but could still probably muster over 800 ships at the start of the battle.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h276_125-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h276-125">
         [121]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On the first day (also the first of the Battle of Thermopylae), the Persians detached 200 seaworthy ships, which were sent to sail around the eastern coast of
       <a href="/wiki/Euboea" title="Euboea">
        Euboea
       </a>
       . These ships were to round Euboea and block the line of retreat for the Allied fleet.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h276_125-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h276-125">
         [121]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Meanwhile, the Allies and the remaining Persians engaged in the late afternoon, the Allies having the better of the engagement and capturing 30 vessels.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h276_125-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-h276-125">
         [121]
        </a>
       </sup>
       That evening, another storm occurred, wrecking the majority of the Persian detachment which had been sent around Euboea.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
        <a href="#cite_note-126">
         [122]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trireme.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="254" data-file-width="522" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Trireme.jpg/300px-Trireme.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Trireme.jpg/450px-Trireme.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7a/Trireme.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trireme.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Greek trireme
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       On the second day of the battle, news reached the Allies that their lines of retreat were no longer threatened; they therefore resolved to maintain their position. They staged a hit-and-run attack on some
       <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">
        Cilician
       </a>
       ships, capturing and destroying them.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127">
        <a href="#cite_note-127">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On the third day, however, the Persian fleet attacked the Allies lines in full force. In a day of savage fighting, the Allies held on to their position, but suffered severe losses
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VIII16_128-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-VIII16-128">
         [124]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (half the Athenian fleet was damaged
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129">
        <a href="#cite_note-129">
         [125]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ); nevertheless, the Allies inflicted equal losses on the Persian fleet.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VIII16_128-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-VIII16-128">
         [124]
        </a>
       </sup>
       That evening, the Allies received news of the fate of Leonidas and the Allies at Thermopylae. Since the Allied fleet was badly damaged, and since it no longer needed to defend the flank of Thermopylae, they retreated from Artemisium to the island of
       <a href="/wiki/Salamis_Island" title="Salamis Island">
        Salamis
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130">
        <a href="#cite_note-130">
         [126]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="September_480_BC:_Salamis">
        September 480 BC: Salamis
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: September 480 BC: Salamis">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Salamis" title="Battle of Salamis">
        Battle of Salamis
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Victory at Thermopylae meant that all Boeotia fell to Xerxes; the two cities that had resisted him,
       <a href="/wiki/Thespiae" title="Thespiae">
        Thespiae
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Plataea" title="Plataea">
        Plataea
       </a>
       , were captured and razed. Attica was also left open to invasion, and the remaining population of Athens was thus evacuated, with the aid of the Allied fleet, to Salamis.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131">
        <a href="#cite_note-131">
         [127]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Peloponnesian Allies began to prepare a defensive line across the Isthmus of Corinth, building a wall, and demolishing the road from
       <a href="/wiki/Megara" title="Megara">
        Megara
       </a>
       , thereby abandoning Athens to the Persians.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132">
        <a href="#cite_note-132">
         [128]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Athens thus fell; the small number of Athenians who had barricaded themselves on the
       <a href="/wiki/Acropolis" title="Acropolis">
        Acropolis
       </a>
       were eventually defeated, and Xerxes then ordered Athens to be torched.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133">
        <a href="#cite_note-133">
         [129]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Persians had now captured most of Greece, but Xerxes had perhaps not expected such defiance from the Greeks; his priority was now to complete the war as quickly as possible;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h327_134-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h327-134">
         [130]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the huge invasion force could not be supplied indefinitely, and probably Xerxes did not wish to be at the fringe of his empire for so long.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h308_135-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h308-135">
         [131]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Thermopylae had shown that a frontal assault against a well defended Greek position had little chance of success;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h310_136-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h310-136">
         [132]
        </a>
       </sup>
       with the Allies now dug in across the isthmus, there was therefore little chance of the Persians conquering the rest of Greece by land. However, if the isthmus's defensive line could be outflanked, the Allies could be defeated.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holland,_p294_137-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Holland,_p294-137">
         [133]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L157_138-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-L157-138">
         [134]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Such an outflanking of the isthmus required the use of the Persian navy, and thus the neutralisation of the Allied navy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h308_135-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h308-135">
         [131]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L157_138-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-L157-138">
         [134]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In summary, if Xerxes could destroy the Allied navy, he would be in a strong position to force a Greek surrender; this seemed the only hope of concluding the campaign in that season.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h308_135-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-h308-135">
         [131]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Conversely by avoiding destruction, or as Themistocles hoped, by destroying the Persian fleet, the Greeks could avoid conquest. In the final reckoning, both sides were prepared to stake everything on a naval battle, in the hope of decisively altering the course of the war.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h303_139-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h303-139">
         [135]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_salamis.png">
         <img alt="Battle of salamis.png" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="556" data-file-width="741" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Battle_of_salamis.png/300px-Battle_of_salamis.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Battle_of_salamis.png/450px-Battle_of_salamis.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Battle_of_salamis.png/600px-Battle_of_salamis.png 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_salamis.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Thus it was that the Allied fleet remained off the coast of Salamis into September, despite the imminent arrival of the Persians. Even after Athens fell to the advancing Persian army, the Allied fleet still remained off the coast of Salamis, trying to lure the Persian fleet to battle.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h303_139-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h303-139">
         [135]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140">
        <a href="#cite_note-140">
         [136]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Partly as a result of subterfuge on the part of Themistocles, the navies finally engaged in the cramped Straits of Salamis.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h310_136-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h310-136">
         [132]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There, the large Persian numbers were an active hindrance, as ships struggled to manoeuvre and became disorganised.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VIII89_141-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-VIII89-141">
         [137]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Seizing the opportunity, the Greek fleet attacked, and scored a decisive victory, sinking or capturing at least 200 Persian ships, and thus ensuring the Peloponnesus would not be outflanked.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h320_142-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h320-142">
         [138]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       According to Herodotus, after this loss Xerxes attempted to build a causeway across the straits to attack Salamis (although
       <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">
        Strabo
       </a>
       and Ctesias place this attempt before the battle). In any case this project was soon abandoned. With the Persians' naval superiority removed, Xerxes feared that the Greeks might sail to the Hellespont and destroy the pontoon bridges.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143">
        <a href="#cite_note-143">
         [139]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to Herodotus, Mardonius volunteered to remain in Greece and complete the conquest with a hand-picked group of troops, while advising Xerxes to retreat to Asia with the bulk of the army.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144">
        <a href="#cite_note-144">
         [140]
        </a>
       </sup>
       All of the Persian forces abandoned Attica, with Mardonius over-wintering in Boeotia and Thessaly.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h327_134-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h327-134">
         [130]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Athenians were thus able to return to their burnt-out city for the winter.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h327_134-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-h327-134">
         [130]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span id="Autumn.2Fwinter_480.2F479_BC">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Autumn/winter_480/479_BC">
        Autumn/winter 480/479 BC
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Autumn/winter 480/479 BC">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
