        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Siege_of_Potidaea">
        Siege of Potidaea
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Siege of Potidaea">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Herodotus tells us that a Persian general,
       <a href="/wiki/Artabazos_I_of_Phrygia" title="Artabazos I of Phrygia">
        Artabazus
       </a>
       , having escorted Xerxes to the Hellespont with 60,000 men, began the return journey to Mardonius in Thessaly.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VIII126_145-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-VIII126-145">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, as he neared
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pallene,_Chalcidice" title="Pallene, Chalcidice">
        Pallene
       </a>
       ,
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        "he thought it right that he should enslave the people of
        <a href="/wiki/Potidaea" title="Potidaea">
         Potidaea
        </a>
        , whom he found in revolt."
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       Despite attempts to capture the Greek city by treachery,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VIII128_146-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-VIII128-146">
         [142]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the Persians were forced to keep up the siege for three months.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VIII129_147-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-VIII129-147">
         [143]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Then, attempting to use an unusually low tide to attack the town from sea, the Persian army was caught by the returning tide, many drowning and the survivors being attacked by the Potideans in boats.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VIII129_147-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-VIII129-147">
         [143]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Artabazus was thus forced to lift the siege, and return to Mardonius with the remnants of his men.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VIII129_147-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-VIII129-147">
         [143]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Siege_of_Olynthus">
        Siege of Olynthus
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Siege of Olynthus">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       While besieging Potidea, Artabazus also decided to besiege
       <a href="/wiki/Olynthus" title="Olynthus">
        Olynthus
       </a>
       , which was also in revolt.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VIII127_148-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-VIII127-148">
         [144]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The town was held by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bottiaean" title="Bottiaean">
        Bottiaean
       </a>
       tribe, who had been driven out of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">
        Macedon
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VIII127_148-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-VIII127-148">
         [144]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Having taken the town, he massacred the defenders, and handed over the town to the
       <a href="/wiki/Chalkidiki" title="Chalkidiki">
        Chalcidian
       </a>
       people.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VIII127_148-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-VIII127-148">
         [144]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="June_479_BC:_Plataea_and_Mycale">
        June 479 BC: Plataea and Mycale
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: June 479 BC: Plataea and Mycale">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Plataea" title="Battle of Plataea">
        Battle of Plataea
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mycale" title="Battle of Mycale">
        Battle of Mycale
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Over the winter, there seems to have been some tension between the Allies. In particular, the Athenians, who were not protected by the isthmus, but whose fleet were the key to the security of the Peloponnesus, felt hard done by. They demanded an Allied army march north the following year.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h333_149-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h333-149">
         [145]
        </a>
       </sup>
       When the other Allies failed to commit to this, the Athenian fleet probably refused to join the Allied navy in the spring.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h336_150-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h336-150">
         [146]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The navy, now under the command of the Spartan king
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leotychides" title="Leotychides">
        Leotychides
       </a>
       , thus skulked off
       <a href="/wiki/Delos" title="Delos">
        Delos
       </a>
       , while the remnants of the Persian fleet skulked off
       <a href="/wiki/Samos" title="Samos">
        Samos
       </a>
       , both sides unwilling to risk battle.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h336_150-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h336-150">
         [146]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Similarly, Mardonius remained in Thessaly, knowing an attack on the isthmus was pointless, while the Allies refused to send an army outside the Peloponessus.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h333_149-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h333-149">
         [145]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Mardonius moved to break the stalemate, by offering peace, self-government and territorial expansion to the Athenians (with the aim of thereby removing their fleet from the Allied forces), using
       <a href="/wiki/Alexander_I_of_Macedon" title="Alexander I of Macedon">
        Alexander I of Macedon
       </a>
       as an intermediary.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h336_150-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-h336-150">
         [146]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Athenians made sure that a Spartan delegation was on hand to hear the offer, but rejected it.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h336_150-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-h336-150">
         [146]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Athens was thus evacuated again, and the Persians marched south and re-took possession of it.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h336_150-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-h336-150">
         [146]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Mardonius now repeated his offer of peace to the Athenian refugees on Salamis. Athens, along with Megara and Plataea, sent emissaries to Sparta demanding assistance, and threatening to accept the Persian terms if not.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151">
        <a href="#cite_note-151">
         [147]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Spartans, who were at that time celebrating the festival of
       <a href="/wiki/Hyacinth_(mythology)" title="Hyacinth (mythology)">
        Hyacinthus
       </a>
       , delayed making a decision for 10 days.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152">
        <a href="#cite_note-152">
         [148]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, when the Athenian emissaries then delivered an ultimatum to the Spartans, they were amazed to hear that a task force was in fact already marching to meet the Persians.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153">
        <a href="#cite_note-153">
         [149]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Plataea_part_2.PNG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="594" data-file-width="807" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Battle_of_Plataea_part_2.PNG/250px-Battle_of_Plataea_part_2.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Battle_of_Plataea_part_2.PNG/375px-Battle_of_Plataea_part_2.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Battle_of_Plataea_part_2.PNG/500px-Battle_of_Plataea_part_2.PNG 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Plataea_part_2.PNG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The main battle at Plataea. The Greek retreat becomes disorganised, and the Persians cross the Asopus to attack.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       When Mardonius heard that the Allied army was on the march, he retreated into Boeotia, near Plataea, trying to draw the Allies into open terrain where he could use his cavalry.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h339_154-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h339-154">
         [150]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Allied army however, under the command of the Spartan regent
       <a href="/wiki/Pausanias_(general)" title="Pausanias (general)">
        Pausanias
       </a>
       , stayed on high ground above Plataea to protect themselves against such tactics.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h342_155-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h342-155">
         [151]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Mardonius ordered a hit-and-run cavalry attack on the Greek lines,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156">
        <a href="#cite_note-156">
         [152]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but the attack was unsuccessful and the cavalry commander killed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157">
        <a href="#cite_note-157">
         [153]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The outcome prompted the Allies to move to a position nearer the Persian camp, still on high ground.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158">
        <a href="#cite_note-158">
         [154]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As a result, the Allied lines of communication were exposed. The Persian cavalry began to intercept food deliveries and finally managed to destroy the only spring of water available to the Allies.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h342_155-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h342-155">
         [151]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Allied position now undermined, Pausanias ordered a night-time retreat towards their original positions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h342_155-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-h342-155">
         [151]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This went awry, leaving the Athenians, and Spartans and Tegeans isolated on separate hills, with the other contingents scattered further away, near Plataea itself.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h342_155-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-h342-155">
         [151]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Seeing that he might never have a better opportunity to attack, Mardonius ordered his whole army forward.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159">
        <a href="#cite_note-159">
         [155]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h350_160-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h350-160">
         [156]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, as at Thermopylae, the Persian infantry proved no match for the heavily armoured Greek hoplites,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161">
        <a href="#cite_note-161">
         [157]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the Spartans broke through to Mardonius's bodyguard and killed him.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162">
        <a href="#cite_note-162">
         [158]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Persian force thus dissolved in rout; 40,000 troops managed to escape via the road to Thessaly,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163">
        <a href="#cite_note-163">
         [159]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but the rest fled to the Persian camp where they were trapped and slaughtered by the Allies, thus finalising their victory.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h350_160-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h350-160">
         [156]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164">
        <a href="#cite_note-164">
         [160]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On the afternoon of the Battle of Plataea, Herodotus tells us that rumour of the Allied victory reached the Allied navy, at that time off the coast of
       <a href="/wiki/Mycale" title="Mycale">
        Mount Mycale
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">
        Ionia
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165">
        <a href="#cite_note-165">
         [161]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Their morale boosted, the Allied marines fought and won a decisive victory at the Battle of Mycale that same day, destroying the remnants of the Persian fleet.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h357_166-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h357-166">
         [162]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As soon as the Peloponnesians had marched north of the isthmus, the Athenian fleet under
       <a href="/wiki/Xanthippus" title="Xanthippus">
        Xanthippus
       </a>
       had joined up with the rest of the Allied fleet.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h357_166-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h357-166">
         [162]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The fleet, now able to match the Persians, had first sailed to Samos, where the Persian fleet was based.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IX96_167-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IX96-167">
         [163]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Persians, whose ships were in a poor state of repair, had decided not to risk fighting, and instead drew their ships up on the beach under Mycale. An army of 60,000 men had been left there by Xerxes, and the fleet joined with them, building a palisade around the camp to protect the ships.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IX96_167-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-IX96-167">
         [163]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, Leotychides decided to attack the camp with the Allied fleet's marines.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h357_166-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-h357-166">
         [162]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Seeing the small size of the Allied force, the Persians emerged from the camp, but the hoplites again proved superior, and destroyed much of the Persian force.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h357_166-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-h357-166">
         [162]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The ships were abandoned to the Allies, who burnt them, crippling Xerxes' sea power, and marking the ascendancy of the Allied fleet.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h357_166-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-h357-166">
         [162]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath">
        Aftermath
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Aftermath">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:117px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Snake_column_Hippodrome_Constantinople_2007.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3061" data-file-width="1521" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Snake_column_Hippodrome_Constantinople_2007.jpg/115px-Snake_column_Hippodrome_Constantinople_2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Snake_column_Hippodrome_Constantinople_2007.jpg/173px-Snake_column_Hippodrome_Constantinople_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Snake_column_Hippodrome_Constantinople_2007.jpg/230px-Snake_column_Hippodrome_Constantinople_2007.jpg 2x" width="115"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Snake_column_Hippodrome_Constantinople_2007.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Serpent_Column" title="Serpent Column">
          Serpent Column
         </a>
         , a monument dedicated by the victorious Allies
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars" title="Greco-Persian Wars">
        Greco-Persian Wars
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       With the twin victories of Plataea and Mycale, the second Persian invasion of Greece was over. Moreover, the threat of future invasion was abated; although the Greeks remained worried that Xerxes would try again, over time it became apparent that the Persian desire to conquer Greece was much diminished.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h358_168-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h358-168">
         [164]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In many ways Mycale represents the start of a new phase of the conflict,
       <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars#Greek_counterattack_(479–478_BC)" title="Greco-Persian Wars">
        the Greek counterattack
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169">
        <a href="#cite_note-169">
         [165]
        </a>
       </sup>
       After the victory at Mycale, the Allied fleet sailed to the Hellespont to break down the pontoon bridges, but found that this was already done.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IX114_170-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IX114-170">
         [166]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Peloponnesians sailed home, but the Athenians remained to attack the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thracian_Chersonese" title="Thracian Chersonese">
        Chersonesos
       </a>
       , still held by the Persians.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IX114_170-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-IX114-170">
         [166]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Persians in the region, and their allies made for
       <a href="/wiki/Sestos" title="Sestos">
        Sestos
       </a>
       , the strongest town in the region, which the Athenians then laid siege to; after a protracted siege, it fell to the Athenians.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h359_171-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h359-171">
         [167]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Herodotus ended his
       <i>
        Historia
       </i>
       after the
       <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars#Sestos" title="Greco-Persian Wars">
        Siege of Sestos
       </a>
       . Over the next 30 years, the Greeks, primarily the Athenian-dominated
       <a href="/wiki/Delian_League" title="Delian League">
        Delian League
       </a>
       , would expel the Persians from Macedon, Thrace, the Aegean islands and Ionia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h359_171-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h359-171">
         [167]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Peace with Persia came in 449 BC with the
       <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars#Peace_with_Persia" title="Greco-Persian Wars">
        Peace of Callias
       </a>
       , finally ending the half-century of warfare.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h359_171-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-h359-171">
         [167]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Tactical_analysis">
        Tactical analysis
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Tactical analysis">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Hoplite" title="Hoplite">
        Hoplite
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Phalanx" title="Phalanx">
        Phalanx
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Greek style of warfare had been honed over the preceding centuries.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h69_172-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h69-172">
         [168]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L256_173-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-L256-173">
         [169]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It revolved around the
       <a href="/wiki/Hoplite" title="Hoplite">
        hoplite
       </a>
       , members of the middle-classes (the
       <i>
        zeugites
       </i>
       ) who could afford the armour necessary to fight in this manner.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174">
        <a href="#cite_note-174">
         [170]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The hoplite was, by the standards of the time, heavily armoured, with a breastplate (originally bronze, but probably by this stage a more flexible leather version), greaves, a full helmet, and a large round shield (the
       <i>
        aspis
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L256_173-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-L256-173">
         [169]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Hoplites were armed with a long spear (the
       <i>
        doru
       </i>
       ), which was evidently significantly longer than Persian spears, and a sword (the
       <i>
        xiphos
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L256_173-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-L256-173">
         [169]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Hoplites fought in the phalanx formation; the exact details are not completely clear, but it was a close-knit formation, presenting a uniform front of overlapping shields, and spears, to the enemy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h69_172-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h69-172">
         [168]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Properly assembled, the phalanx was a formidable offensive and defensive weapon;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L39_175-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-L39-175">
         [171]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176">
        <a href="#cite_note-176">
         [172]
        </a>
       </sup>
       on occasions when it is recorded to have happened, it took a huge number of light infantry to defeat a relatively small phalanx.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L39_175-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-L39-175">
         [171]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The phalanx was vulnerable to being outflanked by cavalry, if caught on the wrong terrain, however.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177">
        <a href="#cite_note-177">
         [173]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The hoplite's heavy armour and long spears made them excellent troops in hand-to-hand combat
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L256_173-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-L256-173">
         [169]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and gave them significant protection against ranged attacks by light troops and skirmishers. Even if the shield did not stop a missile, there was a reasonable chance the armour would.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L256_173-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-L256-173">
         [169]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hoplite1.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="194" height="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Hoplite1.gif/130px-Hoplite1.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b0/Hoplite1.gif 1.5x" width="130"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hoplite1.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Diagram reconstructing the armament of a Greek hoplite
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Persian infantry used in the invasion were a heterogeneous group drawn from across the empire. However, according to Herodotus, there was at least a general conformity in the type of armour and style of fighting.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L23_178-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-L23-178">
         [174]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The troops were, generally speaking, armed with a bow, 'short spear' and sword, carried a wicker shield, and wore at most a leather jerkin.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L23_178-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-L23-178">
         [174]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179">
        <a href="#cite_note-179">
         [175]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The one exception to this may have been the ethnic Persian troops, who may have worn a corslet of scaled armour.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L23_178-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-L23-178">
         [174]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some of the contingents may have been armed somewhat differently;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L23_178-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-L23-178">
         [174]
        </a>
       </sup>
       for instance, the
       <a href="/wiki/Saka" title="Saka">
        Saka
       </a>
       were renowned axemen.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180">
        <a href="#cite_note-180">
         [176]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The 'elite' contingents of the Persian infantry seem to have been the ethnic Persians,
       <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">
        Medians
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khuzistan" title="Khuzistan">
        Cissians
       </a>
       and the Saka.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L23_178-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-L23-178">
         [174]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The foremost of the infantry were the royal guards, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_Immortals" title="Persian Immortals">
        Immortals
       </a>
       , although they were still armed in the aforementioned style.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L23_178-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-L23-178">
         [174]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h274_181-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-h274-181">
         [177]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Cavalry was provided by the Persians,
       <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">
        Bactrians
       </a>
       , Medes, Cissians and Saka; most of these probably fought as lightly armed missile cavalry.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L23_178-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-L23-178">
         [174]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182">
        <a href="#cite_note-182">
         [178]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The style of fighting used by the Persians was probably to stand off from an enemy, using their bows (or equivalent) to wear down the enemy before closing in to deliver the
       <i>
        coup de grace
       </i>
       with spear and sword.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L23_178-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-L23-178">
         [174]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Archers_frieze_Darius_palace_Louvre_AOD487.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1322" data-file-width="924" height="315" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Archers_frieze_Darius_palace_Louvre_AOD487.jpg/220px-Archers_frieze_Darius_palace_Louvre_AOD487.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Archers_frieze_Darius_palace_Louvre_AOD487.jpg/330px-Archers_frieze_Darius_palace_Louvre_AOD487.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Archers_frieze_Darius_palace_Louvre_AOD487.jpg/440px-Archers_frieze_Darius_palace_Louvre_AOD487.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Archers_frieze_Darius_palace_Louvre_AOD487.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Persian soldiers, possibly Immortals, a frieze in Darius's palace at
         <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">
          Susa
         </a>
         . Silicious glazed bricks, c. 510 BC,
         <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">
          Louvre
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Persians had encountered hoplites in battle before at
       <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt#Battle_of_Ephesus" title="Ionian Revolt">
        Ephesus
       </a>
       , where their cavalry had easily routed the (probably exhausted) Greeks.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183">
        <a href="#cite_note-183">
         [179]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, at the battle of Marathon, the Athenian hoplites had shown their superiority over the Persian infantry, albeit in the absence of any cavalry.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184">
        <a href="#cite_note-184">
         [180]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is therefore slightly surprising that the Persians did not bring any hoplites from the Greek regions, especially Ionia, under their control in Asia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L23_178-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-L23-178">
         [174]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Equally, Herodotus tells us that the Egyptian marines serving in the navy were well armed, and performed well against the Greek marines; yet no Egyptian contingent served in the army.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L256_173-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-L256-173">
         [169]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Persians may not have completely trusted the Ionians and Egyptians, since both had recently revolted against Persian rule. However, if this is the case, then it must be questioned why there were Greek and Egyptian contingents in the navy. The Allies evidently tried to play on the Persian fears about the reliability of the Ionians in Persian service;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VIII22_185-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-VIII22-185">
         [181]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IX98_186-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IX98-186">
         [182]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but, as far as we can tell, both the Ionians and Egyptians performed particularly well for the Persian navy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L23_178-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-L23-178">
         [174]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It may therefore simply be that neither the Ionians nor Egyptians were included in the army because they were serving in the fleet — none of the coastal regions of the Persian empire appear to have sent contingents with the army.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L23_178-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-L23-178">
         [174]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In the two major land battles of the invasion, the Allies clearly adjusted their tactics to nullify the Persian advantage in numbers and cavalry, by occupying the pass at Thermopylae, and by staying on high ground at Plataea.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187">
        <a href="#cite_note-187">
         [183]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At Thermopylae, until the path outflanking the Allied position was revealed, the Persians signally failed to adjust their tactics to the situation, although the position was well chosen to limit the Persian options.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h274_181-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h274-181">
         [177]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188">
        <a href="#cite_note-188">
         [184]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At Plataea, the harassing of the Allied positions by cavalry was a successful tactic, forcing the precipitous (and nearly disastrous) retreat; however, Mardonius then brought about a general melee between the infantry, which resulted in the Persian defeat.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L254_189-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-L254-189">
         [185]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The events at Mycale reveal a similar story; Persian infantry committing themselves to a melee with hoplites, with disastrous results.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h357_166-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-h357-166">
         [162]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It has been suggested that there is little evidence of complex tactics in the Greco-Persian wars.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L258_190-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-L258-190">
         [186]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, as simple as the Greek tactics were, they played to their strengths; the Persians however, may have seriously underestimated the strength of the hoplite, and their failure to adapt to facing the Allied infantry contributed to the eventual Persian defeat.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L23_178-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-L23-178">
         [174]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L258_190-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-L258-190">
         [186]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L258_190-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-L258-190">
         [186]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Strategic_analysis">
        Strategic analysis
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Strategic analysis">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       At the beginning of the invasion, it is clear that the Persians held most advantages.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L248_191-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-L248-191">
         [187]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Regardless of its actual size, it is clear that the Persians had brought an overwhelming number of troops and ships to Greece.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-souza_1-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-souza-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192">
        <a href="#cite_note-192">
         [188]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Persians had a unified command system, and everyone was answerable to the king.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193">
        <a href="#cite_note-193">
         [189]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They had a hugely efficient bureaucracy, which allowed them to undertake remarkable feats of planning.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h213_24-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-h213-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194">
        <a href="#cite_note-194">
         [190]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195">
        <a href="#cite_note-195">
         [191]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Persian generals had significant experience of warfare over the 80 years in which the Persian empire had been established.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196">
        <a href="#cite_note-196">
         [192]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Furthermore, the Persians excelled in the use of intelligence and diplomacy in warfare, as shown by their (nearly successful) attempts to divide-and-conquer the Greeks.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197">
        <a href="#cite_note-197">
         [193]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198">
        <a href="#cite_note-198">
         [194]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199">
        <a href="#cite_note-199">
         [195]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Greeks, by comparison, were fragmented, with only 30 or so city-states actively opposing the Persian invasion; even those were prone to quarrel with each other.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200">
        <a href="#cite_note-200">
         [196]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They had little experience of large-scale warfare, being largely restricted to small-scale local warfare,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201">
        <a href="#cite_note-201">
         [197]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and their commanders were chosen primarily on the basis of the political and social standing, rather than because of any experience or expertise.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202">
        <a href="#cite_note-202">
         [198]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As Lazenby therefore asks:
       <i>
        "So why did the Persians fail?"
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L248_191-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-L248-191">
         [187]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Persian strategy for 480 BC was probably to simply progress through Greece in overwhelming force.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L254_189-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-L254-189">
         [185]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The cities in any territory that the army passed through would be forced to submit or risk destruction; and indeed this happened with the Thessalian, Locrian and Phocian cities who initially resisted the Persians but then were forced to submit as the Persians advanced.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203">
        <a href="#cite_note-203">
         [199]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204">
        <a href="#cite_note-204">
         [200]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Conversely, the Allied strategy was probably to try and stop the Persian advance as far north as possible, and thus prevent the submission of as many potential Allies as possible.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L248_191-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-L248-191">
         [187]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Beyond this, the Allies seem to have realised that given the Persians' overwhelming numbers, they had little chance in open battle, and thus they opted to try to defend geographical bottle-necks, where the Persian numbers would count for less.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L248_191-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-L248-191">
         [187]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The whole Allied campaign for 480 BC can be seen in this context. Initially they attempted to defend the
       <a href="/wiki/Vale_of_Tempe" title="Vale of Tempe">
        Tempe pass
       </a>
       to prevent the loss of
       <a href="/wiki/Thessaly" title="Thessaly">
        Thessaly
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L248_191-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-L248-191">
         [187]
        </a>
       </sup>
       After they realised that they could not defend this position, they chose the next-most northerly position, the Thermopylae/Artemisium axis.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L248_191-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-L248-191">
         [187]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Thermopylae" title="Battle of Thermopylae">
        Allied performance at Thermopylae
       </a>
       was initially effective; however, the failure to properly guard the path that outflanked Thermopylae undermined their strategy, and led to defeat.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L248_191-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-L248-191">
         [187]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At Artemisium the fleet also scored some successes, but withdrew due to the losses they had sustained, and since the defeat of Thermopylae made the position irrelevant.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205">
        <a href="#cite_note-205">
         [201]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Thus far, the Persian strategy had succeeded, while the Allied strategy, though not a disaster, had failed.
      </p>
      <p>
       The defence of the
       <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Corinth" title="Isthmus of Corinth">
        Isthmus of Corinth
       </a>
       by the Allies changed the nature of the war. The Persians did not attempt to attack the isthmus by land, realising they probably could not breach it.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206">
        <a href="#cite_note-206">
         [202]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207">
        <a href="#cite_note-207">
         [203]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This essentially reduced the conflict to a naval one.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L248_191-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-L248-191">
         [187]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Themistocles now proposed what was in hindsight the strategic masterstroke in the Allied campaign; to lure the Persian fleet to
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Salamis" title="Battle of Salamis">
        battle in the straits of Salamis
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L248_191-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-L248-191">
         [187]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208">
        <a href="#cite_note-208">
         [204]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, as successful as this was, there was no need for the Persians to fight at Salamis to win the war; it has been suggested that the Persians were either overconfident, or overeager to finish the campaign.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L248_191-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-L248-191">
         [187]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209">
        <a href="#cite_note-209">
         [205]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Thus, the Allied victory at Salamis must at least partially be ascribed to a Persian strategic blunder.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L248_191-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-L248-191">
         [187]
        </a>
       </sup>
       After Salamis, the Persian strategy changed. Mardonius sought to exploit dissensions between the Allies in order to fracture the alliance.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L254_189-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-L254-189">
         [185]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In particular, he sought to win over the Athenians, which would leave the Allied fleet unable to oppose Persian landings on the Peloponnesus.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h333_149-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-h333-149">
         [145]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although Herodotus tells us that Mardonius was keen to fight a decisive battle, his actions in the run-up to Plataea are not particularly consistent with this.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L248_191-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-L248-191">
         [187]
        </a>
       </sup>
       He seems to have been willing to accept battle on his terms, but he waited either for the Allies to attack, or for the alliance to collapse ignominiously.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L248_191-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-L248-191">
         [187]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Allied strategy for 479 BC was something of a mess; the Peloponnesians only agreed to march north in order to save the alliance, and it appears that the Allied leadership had little idea how to force a battle that they could win.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L248_191-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-L248-191">
         [187]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was the botched attempt to retreat from Plataea that finally delivered the Allies battle on their terms.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L254_189-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-L254-189">
         [185]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Mardonius may have been overeager for victory; there was no need to attack the Allies, and by doing so he played to the main Allied tactical strength, combat in the melee.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L254_189-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-L254-189">
         [185]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Allied victory at Plataea can also therefore be seen as partially the result of a Persian mistake.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L254_189-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-L254-189">
         [185]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Thus, the Persian failure may be seen partly as a result of two strategic mistakes that handed the Allies tactical advantages, and resulted in decisive defeats for the Persians.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L254_189-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-L254-189">
         [185]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Allied success is often seen as the result of "
       <i>
        free men fighting for their freedom
       </i>
       ".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210">
        <a href="#cite_note-210">
         [206]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This may have played a part, and certainly the Greeks seem to have interpreted their victory in those terms.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211">
        <a href="#cite_note-211">
         [207]
        </a>
       </sup>
       One crucial factor in the Allied success was that, having formed an alliance, however fractious, they remained true to it, despite the odds.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L254_189-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-L254-189">
         [185]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There appear to have been many occasions when the alliance seemed in doubt, but ultimately it withstood; and while this alone did not defeat the Persians, it meant that even after the occupation of most of Greece, the Allies were not themselves defeated.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L248_191-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-L248-191">
         [187]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This is exemplified by the remarkable fact that the citizens of Athens, Thespiae and Plataea chose to carry on fighting from exile rather than submit to the Persians.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212">
        <a href="#cite_note-212">
         [208]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ultimately, the Allies succeeded because they avoided catastrophic defeats,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L248_191-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-L248-191">
         [187]
        </a>
       </sup>
       stuck to their alliance,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L254_189-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-L254-189">
         [185]
        </a>
       </sup>
       took advantage of Persian mistakes,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L254_189-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-L254-189">
         [185]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and because in the hoplite they possessed an advantage (perhaps their only real advantage at the start of the conflict), which, at Plataea, allowed them to destroy the Persian invasion force.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L256_173-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-L256-173">
         [169]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Significance">
        Significance
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Significance">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greek-Persian_duel.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="859" data-file-width="868" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Greek-Persian_duel.jpg/220px-Greek-Persian_duel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Greek-Persian_duel.jpg/330px-Greek-Persian_duel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Greek-Persian_duel.jpg/440px-Greek-Persian_duel.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greek-Persian_duel.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Greek hoplite and Persian warrior depicted fighting. Ancient kylix, 5th century BC.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The second Persian invasion of Greece was an event of major significance in European history. A large number of historians hold that, had Greece been conquered, the Ancient Greek culture that lies at the basis of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_civilization" title="Western civilization">
        Western civilization
       </a>
       would have never developed (and by extension Western civilization itself).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanson2_213-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-hanson2-213">
         [209]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214">
        <a href="#cite_note-214">
         [210]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215">
        <a href="#cite_note-215">
         [211]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While this may be an exaggeration, it is clear that even at the time the Greeks understood that something very significant had happened.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216">
        <a href="#cite_note-216">
         [212]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Militarily, there was not much in the way of tactical or strategic innovation during the Persian invasion, one commentator suggesting it was something of "a soldier's war" (i.e., it was the soldiers rather than generals that won the war).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217">
        <a href="#cite_note-217">
         [213]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Thermopylae is often used as a good example of the use of terrain as a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Force_multiplier" title="Force multiplier">
        force multiplier
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usarmy_218-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-usarmy-218">
         [214]
        </a>
       </sup>
       while Themistocles's ruse before Salamis is a good example of the use of deception in warfare. The major lesson of the invasion, reaffirming the events at the Battle of Marathon, was the superiority of the hoplite in close-quarters fighting over the more-lightly armed Persian infantry.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h358_168-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-h358-168">
         [164]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L256_173-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-L256-173">
         [169]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Taking on this lesson, the Persian empire would later, after the
       <a href="/wiki/Peloponnesian_War" title="Peloponnesian War">
        Peloponnesian War
       </a>
       , start recruiting and relying on Greek mercenaries.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219">
        <a href="#cite_note-219">
         [215]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
